reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a standard 2D convolution operation with a square input and square kernel.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the square convolution kernel.
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, (kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 16
out_channels = 128
kernel_size = 3
width = 1024
height = 1024

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size]  # Provide in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size for initialization

Register dependency latency With 2304 independent accumulators? No, we have 16 accumulators. The FMAs for different ic/kh/kw values have a dependency on the same accumulator. For memory: Each block loads: - Input: 18*18*16*4 bytes = 20.7 KB - Weight: 16*16*9*4 bytes = 9.2 KB Total per block: ~30 KB For 524K blocks = 15.7 GB of memory traffic At 3.35 TB/s, 15.7 GB takes 4.7 ms. But this is from shared memory, which is much faster. Global memory reads are: - Input: 16 * 16 * 1024 * 1024 * 4 = 1 GB (read once, since tiles don't overlap much with padding) - Weight: 128 * 16 * 9 * 4 = 73 KB - Output: 16 * 128 * 1022 * 1022 * 4 = 8.5 GB Total global: ~9.5 GB At 3.35 TB/s = 2.8 ms for memory-bound. Given that compute is 9.2 ms and memory is 2.8 ms, this is compute-bound. The kernel needs to maximize FMA throughput. Now, with the sequential accumulator dependency, the throughput is limited by the latency of the dependency chain. For 16 accumulators, each with 144 dependent ops, if FMA latency is 4 cycles, then to fully utilize the FMA units, we need at least enough warps to cover this latency. On H100, each SM has many warp schedulers. If each thread has 16 independent accumulators (for 16 output channels), and the inner loop of 144 iterations can be pipelined... actually no, all 144 iterations target the SAME 16 accumulators. So there's a 4-cycle dependency between consecutive iterations. But wait, the compiler can unroll and interleave! If pragma unroll is used, the compiler might interleave multiple FMAs targeting different accumulators. Actually, since the inner loop over kh, kw, ic gives us 144 iterations, and each iteration does 16 FMAs to 16 different accumulators. The 16 FMAs within an iteration are independent! So: For iteration i: FMA(accum[0], in[i], w[i,0]), FMA(accum[1], in[i], w[i,1]), ..., FMA(accum[15], in[i], w[i,15]) These 16 FMAs are independent of each other. But iteration i+1 depends on iteration i's results for the same accumulators. So the dependency chain is only within each accumulator. With 16 independent accumulators, we can overlap the latency. FMA latency is ~4 cycles. We'd need 4 * 2 = 8 independent FMAs in flight to fully utilize 1 FMA pipe per warp? Actually, modern GPUs have multiple FMA units per SM and within a warp, multiple independent FMAs can be issued to different execution units. For FP32 on H100, a warp of 32 threads can issue many FMAs. If each thread does 16 FMAs, that's 512 FMAs per warp. With sufficient instruction-level parallelism, throughput should be good. However, the key issue is the loop-carried dependency on accumulators. For throughput, we'd want many independent operations. One solution: Have each thread compute MULTIPLE spatial pixels, creating more independent accumulators. If each thread computes 2x2 spatial pixels for 4 output channels: - 2x2 * 4 = 16 accumulators, same as before - But now the inner loop still has 144 iterations - The 16 accumulators are still independent within each iteration So the number of independent accumulators is the same. The difference is that for 2x2 pixels, the input values differ but the weights are the same. So the FMAs are still independent. Wait, actually the number of accumulators doesn't change - we still have the same ILP. Unless we compute multiple pixels for the SAME output channel, which creates more accumulators for that channel. If a thread computes 4 pixels for 4 channels: 16 accumulators. If a thread computes 1 pixel for 16 channels: 16 accumulators. Same ILP. But for 2x2 pixels, each pixel needs a different input but same weights. The FMAs targeting different pixels are independent. So with mix of channel and pixel parallelism, we have the same number of independent accumulators. To increase ILP, we need each thread to compute MORE outputs. Like 1 pixel for 32 channels (32 accumulators) or 2x2 pixels for 8 channels (32 accumulators). But adding more accumulators uses more registers. H100 allows 255 registers per thread, and 32 floats use 32 registers - very reasonable. Let me consider computing more output channels per thread. If tile_oc=32, each thread computes 32 outputs. But then weight shared memory = 32*16*9*4 = 18.4 KB, and input = 20.7 KB, total = 39 KB. Still OK. Each thread then does 32 * 144 = 4608 FMAs with 32 independent accumulators. Blocks = 16 * (128/32) * 64 * 64 = 131,072 blocks. At 32 accumulators, we have better ILP. Let me go with this! Tile config: - TILE_H = 16 - TILE_W = 16 - TILE_OC = 32 Block = 16x16 = 256 threads Each thread computes 1 pixel for 32 output channels. Shared memory: - Input: 18*18*16*4 = 20,736 bytes - Weight: 32*16*9*4 = 18,432 bytes - Total: 39,168 bytes = 38.2 KB < 227 KB limit ✓ Register estimate: - 32 accumulators - A few temporaries for loading - Total maybe ~40 registers, well under 255 ✓ Number of blocks: - N=16, OC/TILE_OC=4, OH/TILE_H=64, OW/TILE_W=64 - Total: 16 * 4 * 64 * 64 = 262,144 blocks - Blocks per SM: 262,144 / 132 ≈ 1986 blocks/SM This should provide excellent occupancy and latency hiding. Actually, can we fit even more? TILE_OC = 64: - Weight shared = 64*16*9*4 = 36,864 bytes - Input shared = 20,736 bytes - Total = 57,600 bytes = 56.25 KB. Still OK. Blocks = 16 * 2 * 64 * 64 = 131,072 blocks Each thread has 64 accumulators = 64 registers just for that. Plus other regs, maybe ~80 total. Still under 255. Still well under limit. - Blocks = 16 * 1 * 64 * 64 = 65,536 blocks - Each thread 128 accumulators. That's 128 registers. Plus some for indexes/loads. Maybe ~150 total. Still OK. 65,536 blocks for 132 SMs = ~496 blocks/SM. This is getting lower but should still be enough. If we do TILE_OC=128, each block computes a 16x16x128 output tile = 32,768 values. Each thread computes 128 values. FMAs per thread = 128 * 144 = 18,432 With 128 accumulators, we have tons of ILP. The FMA units should be very well utilized. Wait, but then each block would need to load 128*16*9 = 18,432 weights into shared memory. With 256 threads, each thread loads 72 weights. That's a lot of loading overhead compared to compute. Also, shared memory for weights is 73KB. If we have a 16x16 thread block computing 16x16x128 outputs, that's a large tile. Actually, let me reconsider the occupancy constraint more carefully. If shared memory is 92KB per block, and each SM has 227KB shared memory, then at most 2 blocks can run on an SM simultaneously. This could limit occupancy. With 2 blocks per SM * 132 SMs = 264 blocks concurrently. 65,536 blocks / 264 = ~248 rounds of block execution. If each block takes some time, total execution time might be significant. With TILE_OC=32 and shared memory 38KB, we could fit up to 5 or 6 blocks per SM (227/38 = 5.97), but limited by other factors. At 4 blocks per SM, that's 528 blocks concurrently. 262,144 blocks / 528 = ~497 rounds. Actually, the number of rounds isn't the right metric. What matters is whether there are enough concurrent warps to hide latency. For TILE_OC=32 with 256 threads/block (8 warps), and 4 blocks/SM = 32 warps/SM. H100 can handle hundreds of warps per SM, so 32 might not be enough to hide all latency. For TILE_OC=128 with 256 threads/block, and 2 blocks/SM = 16 warps/SM. Even fewer warps, but each thread has much more compute, so latency hiding is less critical. And cuDNN is very well tuned. Another insight: For this specific case where IC=16 is small, maybe a specialized kernel that treats IC as float4 (4 channels) would be much faster. But only if we can get good vectorization. With IC=16, reading 16 channels as 4 float4 values is natural. And weight can also be read as float4. The dot product of 4 float4 vectors for input and weight can be computed efficiently. For a single output channel and a single spatial position: out = dot(in[0..15], w[0..15]) for each (kh, kw) With float4: 4 dot-products of float4 = 4 * (4 fma) per (kh, kw). For K=3, that's 9 * 16 = 144 FMAs = 36 float4 operations. But the issue is that weights for different output channels are different, so the float4 dot-product needs to be done per output channel. For 64 output channels, we'd do this 64 times. Actually, let me think about this in terms of matrix multiply. The operation is: - A matrix: [OH*OW, IC*K*K] - implicit im2col - B matrix: [IC*K*K, OC] - weight - C matrix: [OH*OW, OC] - output For K=3, IC=16, this is [M, 144] × [144, 128] where M ≈ 1M. In CUTLASS terms, this is a GEMM with small K. For small-K GEMM, specialized kernels that load the K dimension once per tile and maximize register reuse are used. We could write a kernel where: - Each block computes a [M_tile, OC_tile] output tile - The K=144 dimension is loaded once into shared memory or registers - Actually K=144 is small enough to keep in shared memory or even registers If M_tile = 64 (e.g., 8x8 spatial) and OC_tile = 64: - C tile = 64x64 = 4096 values - A tile = 64x144 = 9,216 floats = 36.9 KB - B tile = 144x64 = 9,216 floats = 36.9 KB Both fit in shared memory! And then it's a straightforward GEMM in shared memory. For M=1,044,484 and M_tile=64, we need ~16,320 tiles in M. For OC=128 and OC_tile=64, we need 2 tiles in OC. Total blocks = 16 * 16,320 * 2 = 522,240 blocks. Each block does a 64x64x144 GEMM in shared memory = 1.18M FMAs. 256 threads = ~4608 FMAs per thread. This is very similar to our direct convolution! The advantage of GEMM formulation is we can optimize the inner loop more easily. But direct convolution might be more efficient because: 1. We don't need to materialize the full im2col A matrix 2. We can load the input tile once instead of loading duplicated values Actually in the GEMM approach with shared memory, the A tile corresponds to an im2col tile. We'd need to load input and expand it to im2col format on the fly. This "on-the-fly" expansion is what implicit GEMM does. For a direct convolution kernel, we're implicitly doing the GEMM by loading the input and accessing the correct elements. I think direct convolution is the right approach. Let me write the kernel. One more consideration: PyTorch by default uses NCHW format. But tensor cores often prefer NHWC. However, for our custom kernel, we stick with NCHW since that's what PyTorch gives us. Let me write a kernel with these parameters: - TILE_H = 8 - TILE_W = 32 - TILE_OC = 32 Why TILE_W=32? This gives better coalescing for output writes. With 32 threads in x dimension, a warp writes 32 contiguous output values. Block = 8x32 = 256 threads. Each thread computes 1 pixel for 32 output channels. Input shared = (8+2)*(32+2)*16 = 10*34*16 = 5440 floats = 21.8 KB Weight shared = 32*16*9 = 4608 floats = 18.4 KB Total = 40.2 KB Blocks: - OH/TILE_H = 1022/8 = 128 tiles - OW/TILE_W = 1022/32 = 32 tiles - N=16, OC/TILE_OC=4 - Total = 16 * 128 * 32 * 4 = 262,144 blocks. Each thread FMAs = 32 * 144 = 4608 This looks good. TILE_W=32 gives clean warp coalescing. TILE_H=8 might be OK. Alternatively, TILE_H=16, TILE_W=16 is more balanced. Let me stick with 16x16 since it's simpler. Actually, let me consider warp shape. With block=16x16, the warp shape depends on how threads are grouped. Typically, threads are linearized as threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x, so warp has 32 consecutive threads. If blockDim.x=16, then a warp covers threadIdx.x=0..15 and threadIdx.y=0..1 (2 rows). For output writes, threads in a warp write 2 rows × 16 columns, which is 32 contiguous floats per row but only 16 per row per warp. With 16x16 outputs and TILE_OC=64, output format is [N, OC, OH, OW]. For a fixed n and oc, OH×OW is row-major. So writing 16 consecutive W values per row is coalesced. But writing different OC channels: output[oc0][h][w] and output[oc1][h][w] are not contiguous (they're OC apart). So we should write all OC values for one pixel sequentially. Each thread computes pixel (h, w) for 64 OCs. If we store these 64 values in local array, then we write them one by one. The writes are at stride OH*OW in output memory. So threads in a warp writing their first OC value are at addresses: `base + (oc0) * OH * OW + h * OW + w` for each thread where w varies by thread. Wait, output is NCHW: [N, OC, OH, OW] Position (n, oc, h, w) is at offset ((n * OC + oc) * OH + h) * OW + w For fixed n, h, and varying w across a warp, and fixed oc, the addresses are contiguous (differ by 1). Good! For varying oc, the addresses differ by OH*OW. So in our kernel, for a given (h, w), we should write oc=0, then oc=1, etc. Each write for a specific oc is coalesced across the warp (since w varies). But writing 64 OCs means 64 writes per thread. If we have 32 accumulators and write sequentially, that's fine. Actually, I want to minimize these writes. With TILE_OC=64, each thread writes 64 floats. 64 writes per thread = 64 * 256 = 16,384 writes per block = 64KB written. At high bandwidth this is fine, but it's more instruction overhead. What if we write with float4? We can write 4 OCs at a time. But as noted, OCs are not contiguous for the same pixel. float4 doesn't help unless we transpose. Between TILE_OC=64 (more ILP but more writes) and TILE_OC=32 (less ILP but fewer writes), let me try 64 and see. Actually, I just realized: H100 supports warp-level primitives. We could potentially use tensor cores or warp-matrix-multiply if we structure this right. But given the small K=144, standard FMA might be better. Let me also think about whether we can use `wmma` or use CUTLASS. Actually, for small K, tensor cores are not efficient because they operate on larger tiles (e.g., 16x16x16). OK, let me write the direct convolution kernel with template parameters for tile sizes, making it easy to tune. Here's my plan: 1. Write a CUDA kernel with TILE_H, TILE_W, TILE_OC as template parameters 2. Use shared memory for input and weights 3. Map blocks to (ow_block, oh_block, batch_and_oc_block) 4. Load input tile and weight tile cooperatively 5. Compute with all threads computing the same oc sequentially (broadcast weights) 6. Write output Let me draft the kernel: ```cuda template <int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC> __global__ void conv2d_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int IC, int OC, int H, int W, int K, int OH, int OW) { // Block mapping int ow_block = blockIdx.x; int oh_block = blockIdx.y; int n = blockIdx.z / (OC / TILE_OC); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % (OC / TILE_OC); int ow_base = ow_block * TILE_W; int oh_base = oh_block * TILE_H; int oc_base = oc_block * TILE_OC; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; int nthreads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; // Shared memory // Layout: s_input[IC][tile_h][tile_w] // s_weight[TILE_OC][IC][K][K] extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_weight = s_mem + IC * (TILE_H + K - 1) * (TILE_W + K - 1); // Load input tile int input_tile_h = TILE_H + K - 1; int input_tile_w = TILE_W + K - 1; int input_tile_size = IC * input_tile_h * input_tile_w; for (int idx = tid; idx < input_tile_size; idx += nthreads) { int ic = idx / (input_tile_h * input_tile_w); int rem = idx % (input_tile_h * input_tile_w); int ih_local = rem / input_tile_w; int iw_local = rem % input_tile_w; int ih = oh_base + ih_local; int iw = ow_base + iw_local; // Load from global memory float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && ic < IC && ih < H && iw < W) { val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_input[idx] = val; } // Load weights int weight_size = TILE_OC * IC * K * K; for (int idx = tid; idx < weight_size; idx += nthreads) { int oc = idx / (IC * K * K); int rem = idx % (IC * K * K); int ic = rem / (K * K); rem = rem % (K * K); int kh = rem / K; int kw = rem % K; float val = 0.0f; if (oc_base + oc < OC) { val = weight[((oc_base + oc) * IC + ic) * K * K + kh * K + kw]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute float accum[TILE_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < TILE_OC; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } int oh = oh_base + ty; int ow = ow_base + tx; if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int ih_local = ty + kh; int iw_local = tx + kw; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { float inp = s_input[(ic * input_tile_h + ih_local) * input_tile_w + iw_local]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { float w = s_weight[((oc * IC + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]; accum[oc] += w * inp; } } } } } // Store output if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { output[(((n * OC + oc_base + oc) * OH) + oh) * OW + ow] = accum[oc]; } } } ``` Wait, there are several issues: 1. **Shared memory bank conflicts**: For `s_input`, if layout is [ic][h][w] and we access with fixed ic, h_local, varying w_local, then adjacent threads (varying tx) read adjacent w values. In shared memory, these are contiguous and map to different banks (assuming 32 banks, each 4 bytes). So bank 0 gets w=0, bank 1 gets w=1, etc. No conflicts! But for `s_weight`, all threads read the same weight for a given (oc, ic, kh, kw) - that's a broadcast, also no conflicts. But different iterations of the `oc` loop read different addresses. This sequential access by all threads is also a broadcast for each oc. Wait, but we're iterating `oc` in the inner loop. For a given (ic, kh, kw), all threads read `w[oc=0]`, then `w[oc=1]`, etc. Each read is a broadcast. But we're reading 64 values sequentially. Since all threads read the same address, no bank conflicts. Actually, there is a problem: in the `for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++)` loop inside `for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++)`, all threads are reading the same weight values but at different times. This is fine - it's sequential broadcast, no conflicts. 2. **Loop order**: The inner loops are kh, kw, ic, oc. For fixed ic, kh, kw, we read one input and iterate over all oc weights. This is good because the input value stays in a register, and weights are read from shared memory (broadcast). Wait, actually if we restructure slightly, the input register can be reused across all oc: ```cpp for kh { for kw { for ic { float in = s_input[...]; for oc = 0..TILE_OC { accum[oc] += in * s_weight[oc][ic][kh][kw]; } } } } ``` This keeps `in` in a register and reuses it for all output channels. Much better! 3. **Register pressure**: With TILE_OC=64, `accum[64]` uses 64 registers. Plus `in`, plus loop variables. This is maybe ~70 registers. Under 255, good. 4. **Output writes**: We write TILE_OC values per thread. If TILE_OC=64, that's 64 stores. But output is only 8.5GB total, and peak write bandwidth is ~1.675 TB/s (half of total), so memory is not the bottleneck. Let me refine the kernel with the loop reordering. Also, I should add vectorized loads where possible. For input loading, since IC * tile_h * tile_w = 16 * 18 * 18 = 5184, we could load using float4 if the indexing allows. But since the test is general (IC might not be multiple of 4 in general), maybe float4 is tricky. For our test, IC=16, so we COULD use float4 for channels, but that's an optimization for later. Let me also check: can we make TILE_OC a template parameter and instantiate it with 64? Yes. Actually, there might be an issue with `float accum[TILE_OC]` when TILE_OC is large. CUDA allows up to 256 bytes of local memory per thread without spilling if compiler decides, but with 64 floats = 256 bytes, this fits in registers if compiler is smart. Actually 64 floats = 256 bytes. H100 registers are 4 bytes each. 64 registers is fine. But with `accum` as local array, the compiler should put it in registers if there's no dynamic indexing. Here we index with constant/unrolled `oc`, so it should be in registers. Wait, I used `#pragma unroll` on the `oc` loop, but dynamic array indexing inside an unrolled loop is resolved at compile time. So `accum[oc]` for each unrolled iteration refers to a specific register. Good. Let me also think about writing output. If TILE_OC=64, writing output sequentially does 64 stores. Could we use float4 for output if we rearrange? No, output for different oc of the same pixel are not contiguous. For 132 SMs with shared mem limiting to maybe 3 blocks each = 396 concurrent blocks. 131K / 396 = 331 rounds. If each round takes ~15 microseconds, that's ~5 ms total. But this is very rough. For comparison, the reference might take ~8-12 ms. So we have a chance! But PyTorch's cuDNN might already be highly optimized for this. To beat it, we need to be very efficient. Let me think about another optimization: Since H=1024, W=1024, OH=1022, OW=1022, we have many threads doing nothing in the last tile of each row/column (since 1022 is not divisible by 16, the last tile has some invalid positions). Actually, 1022 / 16 = 63.875, so 64 tiles. The last tile has positions 1008 to 1023, but valid outputs are only up to 1021. So 1024 - 1022 = 2 rows/cols are invalid in the last tiles. That's a small inefficiency (~3%). Now, the input loading: for the last tiles, we load pixels up to 1023 (which is valid since H=W=1024), but only use them for outputs up to 1021. The extra 2 rows/cols are loaded but not all used. Small inefficiency. Let me write this up. But I want to also consider if we should tune TILE_H, TILE_W, TILE_OC. Let me make a few versions or start with one and iterate. I'll start with TILE_H=16, TILE_W=16, TILE_OC=64. One concern: 131K blocks might not fully saturate the GPU. So 2 blocks on the same SM take longer than 1 block alone, but the total throughput of the SM is fixed. If each SM can do X FMAs per cycle, and we have 132 SMs, then total FMAs / total throughput = time. The number of rounds doesn't directly multiply block time. Actually, 132 SMs * 2 blocks/SM = 264 blocks running at once. 131K blocks / 264 = 496 "waves". Each wave takes time T_wave. Total time = 496 * T_wave. T_wave is the time for a block to finish given shared SM resources. With 256 threads and 64 accumulators, each thread has high ILP, so the block should be compute-bound and take time proportional to FMAs / throughput. If an SM has 128 FP32 FMA units running at 2GHz, that's 256 GFMA/s per SM = 256B FMAs per second. Total FMAs per block per SM = 2 blocks * 256 threads * 9216 FMAs = 4.7M FMAs. Time = 4.7M / 256B/s = 18.4 microseconds. Total time = 496 * 18.4us = 9.1 ms. Very close to theoretical compute limit! But that's assuming perfect utilization and no memory overhead. Actually, here's an even simpler kernel idea: Instead of using shared memory for the entire input tile, what if we stream the computation? Each warp computes a set of output pixels, and for each (kh, kw), we load the required input values and broadcast-multiply with weights. Specifically: - A warp of 32 threads computes 32 output pixels (spanned across spatial positions) - For each output channel group (say 32 OCs), for each (ic, kh, kw): - Load 32 input values (one per thread, coalesced) - Load 32 weights (broadcast) - FMA This is very similar to what cuDNN likely does for small K implicit GEMM. But without tensor cores, it's hard to beat cuDNN. Let me just implement the shared-memory version and tune iteratively. Let me refine the shared memory kernel one more time: ```cuda template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC> __global__ void conv2d_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int IC, int OC, int H, int W, int K, int OH, int OW) { const int in_tile_h = TILE_H + K - 1; const int in_tile_w = TILE_W + K - 1; int n = blockIdx.z / (OC / TILE_OC); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % (OC / TILE_OC); int oh_base = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int ow_base = blockIdx.x * TILE_W; int oc_base = oc_block * TILE_OC; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_weight = s_mem + IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; // Cooperative loading of input tile int input_size = IC * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; int nthreads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; for (int idx = tid; idx < input_size; idx += nthreads) { int ic = idx / (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int rem = idx % (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int ih = oh_base + rem / in_tile_w; int iw = ow_base + rem % in_tile_w; float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && ic < IC && ih < H && iw < W) { val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_input[idx] = val; } // Cooperative loading of weights int weight_size = TILE_OC * IC * K * K; for (int idx = tid; idx < weight_size; idx += nthreads) { int idx2 = idx; int oc = idx2 / (IC * K * K); idx2 %= IC * K * K; int ic = idx2 / (K * K); idx2 %= K * K; int kh = idx2 / K; int kw = idx2 % K; float val = 0.0f; if (oc_base + oc < OC) { val = weight[(((oc_base + oc) * IC + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); int oh = oh_base + ty; int ow = ow_base + tx; float accum[TILE_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < TILE_OC; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int ih = ty + kh; int iw = tx + kw; float inp = s_input[(ih * in_tile_w + iw) * IC + 0]; // Wait, layout? ``` Here's a key design decision: How to lay out shared memory for input? Option 1: [IC][in_tile_h][in_tile_w] (channel-major) Option 2: [in_tile_h][in_tile_w][IC] (spatial-major) For our access pattern: For a given spatial position (ih, iw), we iterate over all IC channels. So spatial-major gives contiguous IC values. This means for a fixed (ih, iw), channels are contiguous. But in the computation, we do: ``` for kh: for kw: for ic: in = s_input[...ic...] for oc: accum[oc] += in * weight[oc][ic][kh][kw] ``` Here, for fixed (kh, kw), we iterate over ic. If layout is [in_tile_h][in_tile_w][IC], then for fixed (ih, iw), ic is contiguous. Good for reading sequential values. But different threads read different (ih, iw), so shared memory access is coalesced across threads. Actually, wait. For fixed (kh, kw), thread (ty, tx) reads input at (ty+kh, tx+kw). So all threads in a block read different (ih, iw) positions. If layout is [h][w][IC], then thread accesses are at different h*w positions, strided by IC. If IC=16, then thread 0 reads at offset0, thread1 at offset16, thread2 at offset32, etc. These map to different banks (since 16 floats = 64 bytes = bank 0, bank 16, bank 0, bank 16...). Wait, 16 floats * 4 bytes = 64 bytes. Shared memory has 32 banks, each 4 bytes. So offset 0 = bank 0, offset 64 = bank 0. So threads 0 and 2 would both access bank 0! This is a bank conflict! To avoid bank conflicts with channel-major layout [IC][h][w]: Thread (ty, tx) reads at ic * tile_h * tile_w + (ty+kh) * tile_w + (tx+kw). For fixed ic, kh, kw: thread accesses are at (ty*tile_w + tx). With tile_w=16, thread 0 (0,0) at 0, thread 1 (0,1) at 1, etc. Adjacent threads access adjacent banks. No conflict! This is ideal. But for iterating over ic, we go from ic=0 to ic=15. Each ic is offset by tile_h*tile_w*4 bytes = 18*18*4 = 1296 bytes. 1296 / 128 = 10.125? Let me recalculate. Actually, bank = (address_in_bytes / 4) % 32. For [ic][h][w] layout, address = (ic * tile_h * tile_w + h * tile_w + w) * 4. bank = (ic * tile_h * tile_w + h * tile_w + w) % 32. For fixed h, w and varying ic: bank changes by tile_h * tile_w mod 32 = 18*18 mod 32 = 324 mod 32 = 4. So consecutive ic values are in banks 4 apart (0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28, 0...). No conflicts for consecutive ic! Good. For different threads at fixed ic, h=ty+kh, w=tx+kw: bank difference between thread (ty, tx) and (ty, tx+1) = 1. Adjacent banks, no conflict. This layout [ic][h][w] seems excellent. What about [h][w][ic] layout? address = (h * tile_w * IC + w * IC + ic) * 4. For fixed h, w and adjacent threads (tx, tx+1): address difference = IC*4 = 64 bytes = bank difference = 16. Wait, no: adjacent tx means w differs by 1. address difference = IC * 4 = 64 bytes. bank difference = 16. So thread 0 is bank 0, thread 1 is bank 16, thread 2 is bank 0 (since IC=16, 2*IC=32 ≡ 0 mod 32). So threads 0 and 2 conflict! This is BAD for [h][w][ic] layout with IC=16. So [ic][h][w] is definitely the right layout. Now for weights: [oc][ic][kh][kw] or [oc][kh][kw][ic]. We access weights as: for fixed oc_block, all threads iterate over oc=0..TILE_OC-1, and within each oc, iterate over ic, kh, kw. If layout is [oc][ic][kh][kw], then for fixed oc, ic, kh, kw, all threads read the same address = broadcast, no conflict. For fixed kh, kw, ic, and iterating over oc: addresses differ by IC*K*K elements. IC*K*K = 16*9 = 144. 144 mod 32 = 16. So consecutive oc are 16 banks apart. For TILE_OC=64, we get oc=0 at bank X, oc=1 at bank X+16, oc=2 at bank X, oc=3 at bank X+16. For a warp reading these sequentially, half the threads in warp read same bank at a time? Actually all threads read the same address for a given oc. Then for next oc, all threads read the next address. No conflict since it's broadcast each time, just with a stride. Actually, since ALL threads read the SAME address for each (oc, ic, kh, kw), it's always a broadcast. No bank conflicts regardless of layout! But for loading weights from global memory to shared memory, we want coalescing. For [oc][ic][kh][kw], adjacent threads in the loading loop read adjacent memory if they load adjacent (oc, ic, kh, kw). Weight in global memory is stored as [OC][IC][K][K]. So loading sequentially is naturally coalesced. OK, so layout [ic][h][w] for input and [oc][ic][kh][kw] for weights. Let me rewrite the kernel with these layouts. For input: ``` s_input[ic * in_tile_h * in_tile_w + ih * in_tile_w + iw] ``` For weights: ``` s_weight[oc * IC * K * K + ic * K * K + kh * K + kw] ``` And in the compute loop: ``` for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int ih = ty + kh; int iw = tx + kw; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { float in = s_input[ic * in_tile_h * in_tile_w + ih * in_tile_w + iw]; for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { float w = s_weight[oc * IC * K * K + ic * K * K + kh * K + kw]; accum[oc] += w * in; } } } } ``` Wait, I should move `ic` to the outermost of the inner loops to maximize `in` reuse: ``` for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { int ih = ty + kh; int iw = tx + kw; for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { float in = s_input[ic * in_tile_h * in_tile_w + ih * in_tile_w + iw]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { float w = s_weight[oc * IC * K * K + ic * K * K + kh * K + kw]; accum[oc] += w * in; } } } } ``` Actually, a better loop order for register reuse of weights? No, weights for different oc are different, so they can't be reused in a register. But `in` is reused for all oc. So keeping `in` in a register across the oc loop is optimal. Can we swap the ic and kh, kw loops for better memory locality? If we iterate ic outermost: ``` for (int ic = 0; ic < IC; ic++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < K; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < K; kw++) { float in = s_input[...]; for (oc...) ... } } } ``` For a given ic, we touch 9 spatial locations. With [ic][h][w] layout, these 9 locations are close together (within a 3x3 window). But they span multiple rows. The locality depends on the cache structure of shared memory, which is divided into banks but otherwise linear. Actually, for kh and kw as inner loops, for each (ic, kh, kw), we load one float. The total working set for ic=0 is 3x3=9 floats = 36 bytes. This fits in a small number of cache lines. So locality is good either way. Let me keep kh, kw outer to exploit the possibility of unrolling K=3. Actually, since K=3 is small, `#pragma unroll` on kh and kw should unroll the 9 iterations. This gives us: ``` #pragma unroll for (kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { #pragma unroll for (ic = 0; ic < 16; ic++) { // if IC=16 and we can specialize float in = s_input[...]; #pragma unroll for (oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { accum[oc] += w[oc][ic][kh][kw] * in; } } } } ``` With K=3 and IC=16 fully unrolled, this is a straight line of 9*16*(TILE_OC+1) operations. For TILE_OC=64, that's 144 * 65 = 9360 operations per thread (including loads and FMAs). All loads from shared memory. This is very efficient. Wait, if IC=16 is unrolled, the compiler might inline the loads. But we pass IC as a runtime parameter. To force unrolling, we'd need either a template parameter or to hardcode 16. For now, I'll let the compiler handle it with `#pragma unroll` and hope it unrolls small loops. But with a runtime variable, `#pragma unroll` without a number might not fully unroll IC. I could add: ``` if (IC == 16 && K == 3) { // Specialized code with hardcoded 16 and 3 } else { // Generic code } ``` Let me do this to help the compiler generate optimal code for the test parameters. But the compiler only sees IC as a variable. For template parameters, I can do: ``` template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC, int K_CONST, int IC_CONST> __global__ void conv2d_kernel(...) ``` But we'd only instantiate when K_CONST == K and IC_CONST == IC. We can check at runtime and fall back to generic or instantiate common cases. For simplicity in iteration, let me first write a generic kernel and see if it's fast enough. If not, I'll specialize. Actually, I realize that `#pragma unroll` with a runtime loop bound might not work. The standard syntax is `#pragma unroll N` where N is a compile-time constant. But for a loop `for (int i = 0; i < K; i++)`, if K is passed as a parameter, `#pragma unroll` (without N) might fully unroll very small loops, but it's compiler-dependent. Alright, here's my final kernel structure for the first attempt: ```cuda template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC, int K_VAL, int IC_VAL> __global__ void conv2d_kernel_specialized( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int OC, int H, int W, int OH, int OW) { const int in_tile_h = TILE_H + K_VAL - 1; const int in_tile_w = TILE_W + K_VAL - 1; int n = blockIdx.z / ((OC + TILE_OC - 1) / TILE_OC); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % ((OC + TILE_OC - 1) / TILE_OC); int oh_base = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int ow_base = blockIdx.x * TILE_W; int oc_base = oc_block * TILE_OC; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; int nthreads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; __shared__ float s_input[IC_VAL * in_tile_h * in_tile_w]; __shared__ float s_weight[TILE_OC * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL]; // Load input #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < IC_VAL * in_tile_h * in_tile_w; idx += nthreads) { int ic = idx / (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int rem = idx % (in_tile_h * in_tile_w); int ih = oh_base + rem / in_tile_w; int iw = ow_base + rem % in_tile_w; float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && ih < H && iw < W) { val = input[((n * IC_VAL + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_input[idx] = val; } // Load weights #pragma unroll for (int idx = tid; idx < TILE_OC * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL; idx += nthreads) { int oc = idx / (IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL); int rem = idx % (IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL); int ic = rem / (K_VAL * K_VAL); rem = rem % (K_VAL * K_VAL); int kh = rem / K_VAL; int kw = rem % K_VAL; float val = 0.0f; if (oc_base + oc < OC) { val = weight[(((oc_base + oc) * IC_VAL + ic) * K_VAL + kh) * K_VAL + kw]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } __syncthreads(); int oh = oh_base + ty; int ow = ow_base + tx; float accum[TILE_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < TILE_OC; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K_VAL; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K_VAL; kw++) { int ih = ty + kh; int iw = tx + kw; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IC_VAL; ic++) { float in_val = s_input[ic * in_tile_h * in_tile_w + ih * in_tile_w + iw]; If PyTorch uses TF32, the error per MAC is about 1 ulp of TF32 ≈ 2^-10 ≈ 0.001. For 144 MACs, the accumulation error could be ~0.001 * sqrt(144) ≈ 0.012 with random sign, or 0.001 * 144 = 0.144 with same sign. But since weights are random (both positive and negative), errors partially cancel. Expected absolute error is probably closer to 0.001 * sqrt(144) * std(input*weight) ≈ 0.01 or so. An absolute error of 0.01 at output values of ~1-4 would give relative error of 0.25%-1%, which is well above 1e-4. Wait, but I think TF32 tensor cores accumulate in full fp32! The multiplication inputs are rounded to TF32, but the multiply and add are exact in fp32. So the error only comes from the multiplication inputs being rounded. For random inputs in [0,1] and random weights ~N(0, std), the product has some variance. The rounding of inputs to TF32 introduces an error of at most 0.5 ulp(TF32) in each operand. The relative error in each operand is <= 2^-10. For a product, relative error is about 2 * 2^-10 = 2^-9. The absolute error in each product is then about val * 2^-9. For values around 1, absolute error per product is ~0.002. Sum of 144 such products with random sign: the error variance is 144 * (0.002)^2 * variance_of_sign. The standard deviation of the total error is sqrt(144) * 0.002 = 12 * 0.002 = 0.024. So we expect absolute errors around 0.02-0.03. 0.03 is way above 1e-4! The key to beating cuDNN is maximizing FMA unit utilization! For this, we need: 1. Many independent FMAs in flight per SM 2. Good warp scheduling 3. Minimal stalls With 38KB shared memory and 256 threads, we can potentially fit 5+ blocks per SM, which gives 30+ warps. H100 can schedule many warps. But to keep FMA units busy, each warp needs independent FMAs. If a thread has 32 accumulators and does a loop of 32 FMAs per iteration, then within a warp there are 32 threads * 32 FMAs = 1024 independent FMAs per iteration. That's quite a lot! Each cycle, a warp can issue multiple FMAs. On H100, a warp scheduler can issue an FMA instruction every cycle. With 4 warp schedulers, that's 4 * 32 = 128 FMAs per cycle per SM. To saturate 128 FMAs/cycle, we need 128 independent FMAs ready each cycle. With 1024 independent FMAs per iteration, we have 8 cycles worth. This should be sufficient to keep the pipeline full. So I think TILE_H=8, TILE_W=32, TILE_OC=32 should be able to achieve near-peak FMA throughput. Wait, bank conflict when writing TO shared memory during loading is irrelevant (each thread writes its own location). For reading FROM global memory, we need coalesced global loads. Global memory access pattern: Thread 0 reads global at offset for (n, ic=0, h=oh_base+0, w=ow_base+0) Thread 1 reads global at offset for (n, ic=0, h=oh_base+0, w=ow_base+1) ... Thread 33 reads global at offset for (n, ic=0, h=oh_base+1, w=ow_base+0) But thread 0 loads global at: base + ((n*IC + 0) * H + oh_base) * W + ow_base Thread 256 loads global at: base + ((n*IC + ???) * H + h) * W + w If element 256 corresponds to ic=256/340=0 (since 10*34=340), h=256/34=7, w=256%34=18. Wait: ic = idx / 340. idx=256: ic=0, rem=256, h=256/34=7, w=256%34=18. So thread 0 loads (ic=0, h=0, w=0). Thread 256 loads (ic=0, h=7, w=18). These are NOT contiguous in global memory. In fact, they're far apart! This means the loop `for (idx = tid; idx < total; idx += nthreads)` with elements mapped to [ic][h][w] gives poor coalescing for global loads! To fix this, we need to assign threads to contiguous elements in global memory. Global memory layout is [N][IC][H][W]. For a fixed n, elements are contiguous along W, then H, then IC. For the input tile, we want to load from [n][ic][oh_base + ih][ow_base + iw]. Contiguous global memory corresponds to varying iw, then ih, then ic. So for coalesced loads, adjacent threads should load adjacent iw values. With 256 threads, we want thread i to load iw = (i % 34), ih = (i / 34) % 10, ic = i / 340. But 256 threads = 7 full rows (7*34=238) + 18 remaining. Warp 0: threads 0..31 load iw=0..31, ih=0, ic=0. That's 32 contiguous floats. Perfect coalescing. Warp 1: threads 32..63 load iw=32..33 (2 floats), then ih=1, iw=0..29 (30 floats). Not fully contiguous but still within a small range. Actually, global memory coalescing works within 128-byte segments. So as long as threads in a warp access addresses within a 128-byte window, it's coalesced. For threads 32..63: - thread 32: (ic=0, h=0, w=32) - thread 33: (ic=0, h=0, w=33) - thread 34: (ic=0, h=1, w=0) ... Address of (ic, h, w) = base + ic*H*W + h*W + w. thread 32: base + 0 + 0 + 32 = base + 32*4 = base + 128 bytes. thread 33: base + 33*4 = base + 132 bytes. thread 34: base + 1*W + 0 = base + 1024*4 = base + 4096 bytes. Oh no, thread 34 is at base + 4096, which is not contiguous with thread 33 at base + 132. This means warp 1 (threads 32-63) does NOT coalesce! Threads 32 and 33 are close, but thread 34 jumps to a different row. This is a major issue. To fix global memory coalescing, we need adjacent threads to load adjacent W coordinates. One approach: Have each warp load a contiguous 1D slice of the 3D input tile. Map thread 0 to w=0, h=0, ic=0; thread 1 to w=1, h=0, ic=0; etc. When w exceeds tile_w, increment h. When h exceeds tile_h, increment ic. Formally: ``` int idx = tid; // 0 to nthreads-1 int total_per_ic = in_tile_h * in_tile_w = 340; int ic = tid / total_per_ic; int rem = tid % total_per_ic; int h = rem / in_tile_w; int w = rem % in_tile_w; ``` But with 256 threads and 340 elements per ic, warp 0 covers threads 0-31 which are within ic=0, w=0..31, h=0. Contiguous. Good. Warp 1 covers threads 32-63: ic=0, w=32..33 and h=1, w=0..29. Address of thread 32: base + 32*4 = base+128. Address of thread 33: base + 33*4 = base+132. Address of thread 34: base + 1*1024*4 + 0*4 = base+4096. These are far apart. 4096 - 132 = 3964 bytes = 31 cache lines. This is NOT coalesced. To make it coalesced, we should process the entire input tile by having each thread load along the W dimension for a specific (ic, h). But each (ic, h) pair has only 34 elements, and we have 256 threads. So we need to load multiple (ic, h) positions. Better: Have warps load one (ic, h) at a time. Each warp loads 32 contiguous w values for one (ic, h). - Warp 0: (ic=0, h=0, w=0..31) - Warp 1: (ic=0, h=0, w=32..33) and then (ic=0, h=1, w=0..29) But warp 1 still has a gap. Actually, for (ic=0, h=0, w=32..33): that's only 2 elements. We could have warp 1 do (ic=0, h=1, w=0..31). Then the remaining elements (ic=0, h=0, w=32..33) are loaded by thread 32 and 33 of warp 0? No, warp 0 already loaded 0..31. Wait, warp 0 loads 32 elements. So 2 elements (w=32,33) remain for h=0. We could load them with threads 0 and 1 of some warp. Not clean. Let's see: 340 elements per ic. Let's divide by warp size 32. 340 / 32 = 10.625 warps per ic. Not an integer. For the configuration with TILE_H=16, TILE_W=16: Input tile = 18*18 = 324 elements per ic. 324 / 32 = 10.125 warps. Still not integer. For TILE_H=8, TILE_W=16: Input tile = 10*18 = 180 elements per ic. 180 / 32 = 5.625 warps per ic. Hmm. What if we use float4 vectorized loads? With float4 (16 bytes), each thread loads 4 elements. 340 / 4 = 85 float4 loads per ic. With 256 threads / 4 = 64 float4 operations per iteration... no, 256 threads each doing float4 loads = 256*4 = 1024 elements per iteration. If we do: ```cpp for (int idx = tid; idx < total_elems; idx += nthreads) { ... } ``` With nthreads=256 and total_elems=5440, we get 5440/256 = 21.25 iterations. Thread 0: elements 0, 256, 512, ..., 5120. Thread 1: elements 1, 257, 513, ..., 5121. Adjacent threads load adjacent elements! This is NOT contiguous in global memory because of the big stride, but... Wait, for global memory coalescing, what matters is whether threads in a warp access contiguous memory. With `idx = tid + iter * nthreads`: In iteration 0, threads 0..31 load elements 0..31. These are contiguous. Coalesced! In iteration 1, threads 0..31 load elements 256..287. Are these contiguous? Element 256: ic=0, h=7, w=18 Element 257: ic=0, h=7, w=19 ... Element 273: ic=0, h=8, w=0 (wait, 7*34+18=256, 7*34+19=257... 7*34+33=271, then 8*34+0=272). So elements 256..271 are in row 7, columns 18..33. Elements 272..287 are in row 8, columns 0..15. Are these contiguous in global memory? Element (ic, h, w) = base + ic*H*W + h*W + w. h=7, w=18: base + 7*1024 + 18 = base + 7186 h=7, w=19: base + 7187 ... h=7, w=33: base + 7201 h=8, w=0: base + 8192 Oops! Element 272 (h=8, w=0) is at base + 8192, while element 271 (h=7, w=33) is at base + 7201. There's a jump of 991 elements! That's H - 34 = 1024 - 34 = 990. So elements 271 and 272 are NOT contiguous in global memory. So iteration 1 for warp 0 loads: thread 0: base + 7186 thread 1: base + 7187 ... thread 15: base + 7201 thread 16: base + 8192 thread 17: base + 8193 ... This is a TERRIBLE access pattern. The warp would issue 2 transactions: one for 0..15 and one for 16..31. Actually, because coalescing works on 128-byte segments, thread 15 is at base+7201 (+15*4 = +60 bytes from thread 0's address). That's still within one 128-byte segment (base+7168 to base+7295). Thread 16 is at base+8192, which is a different segment. So the warp issues at least 2 loads, and threads 0..15 are in one segment, threads 16..31 in another. But these two segments are far apart. Actually, modern GPUs can handle up to 32 transactions per warp. But each transaction to a different cache line has overhead. This access pattern is really bad! We'd have many cache lines loaded per warp, and most of each cache line would be unused. To fix this, we need adjacent threads to load adjacent memory in global memory. The simplest way is to map threads to contiguous W coordinates, and use a strided mapping that respects the global layout. For the tile [ic][h][w], global memory has w contiguous. So within a row (fixed ic, h), w is contiguous. Across rows (fixed ic, h+1), there's a gap of W - in_tile_w. For a single warp to load contiguous memory, it should load a single row of up to 32 elements, or multiple rows if they're contiguous (they aren't). One strategy: process row by row. Each warp loads elements from one row at a time. - For row h, there are in_tile_w = 34 elements. - Warp has 32 threads. It loads w=0..31. - Remaining 2 elements (w=32,33) are loaded by threads 0..1 of the next warp (or same warp in another iteration). - Then move to row h+1. To implement this cleanly, we can have threads iterate over rows: ```cpp for (int idx = tid; idx < in_tile_h * in_tile_w; idx += nthreads) { int h = idx / in_tile_w; int w = idx % in_tile_w; int ic = ...; } ``` No, this maps adjacent threads to adjacent (h, w) in the tile. For the first warp: thread 0: h=0, w=0 thread 1: h=0, w=1 ... thread 31: h=0, w=31 thread 32: h=0, w=32 Global addresses for threads 0-31: base + h*W + w = base + 0*1024 + 0 to 31. Contiguous! Coalesced! Thread 32: base + 0*1024 + 32 = base + 32. Still contiguous with thread 31! If warp has 32 threads, thread 31 is at base+31*4=base+124. Thread 32 would be in the next warp. So warp 0 (threads 0-31) loads 32 contiguous floats = 128 bytes = perfect coalescing (2 cache lines of 64 bytes each). Warp 1 (threads 32-63): thread 32: h=0, w=32 thread 33: h=0, w=33 thread 34: h=1, w=0 thread 35: h=1, w=1 ... Thread 32: base + 32. Thread 33: base + 33. Thread 34: base + 1024. Thread 35: base + 1025. These are NOT contiguous. Warp 1 loads base+32, base+33, base+1024, base+1025, etc. This will require many transactions. Hmm, the issue is that we're mixing the tail of one row with the start of the next row within the same warp. To fix this, we can process each row with as many full warps as possible, and handle the tail separately. For row length 34: - 32 elements can be loaded by one warp (w=0..31). - Remaining 2 elements (w=32,33) loaded by threads 0..1 of some warp. Not efficient but only 2 elements. So for each row h: ```cpp int w_start = tid % 32; // or something ``` Actually, a cleaner approach: map threads so that each warp handles a full 32-width chunk if possible. For in_tile_w = 34: - Elements 0..31: loaded by warp 0 - Elements 32..33: loaded by threads 0..1 of warp 1 - Elements 34..65: loaded by warp 1... wait, this overlaps. This is getting complicated. Let me instead choose TILE_W such that in_tile_w is a multiple of 32! If K=3, then in_tile_w = TILE_W + 2. For in_tile_w to be a multiple of 32: TILE_W + 2 = 32, 64, 96... TILE_W = 30, 62, 94... If TILE_W = 30: Block = 8x30 = 240 threads. in_tile_w = 32. Perfect multiple! in_tile_h = 10. s_input = 10*32*16*4 = 20,480 bytes. s_weight = 32*16*9*4 = 18,432 bytes. Total = 38,912 bytes = 38 KB. Blocks: - grid.x = (1022 + 29) / 30 = 1052 / 30 = 35 - grid.y = (1022 + 7) / 8 = 128 - grid.z = 16 * 4 = 64 - Total = 35 * 128 * 64 = 286,720 blocks FMAs per thread = 32 * 144 = 4,608. This is very similar to 8x32 config but with 286K blocks instead of 262K, and better coalescing! For input loading: Total elements = 10*32*16 = 5,120. With 240 threads, each thread loads 5,120/240 = 21.33 elements. Thread i loads elements i, i+240, i+480, ..., i+5040. Element mapping: ic = idx / (10*32) = idx / 320, rem = idx % 320, h = rem / 32, w = rem % 32. For iteration 0, thread i (i in {0..31}) has: idx = i, so ic=0, h=0, w=i. Global address = base + w. Contiguous! Coalesced! For iteration 1, idx = i + 240. ic = 240/320 = 0, rem = 240, h = 240/32 = 7, w = 240%32 = 16. So thread i loads (ic=0, h=7, w=16+i). For i=0..15: w=16..31, row 7. Contiguous! Coalesced! For i=16..31: w=32..47, but w only goes up to 31! So w wraps around? No, 16+i for i=16 is 32, but in_tile_w=32, so w=32 is out of bounds! Wait, I miscalculated. idx=256 would be ic=0, h=8, w=0. idx=257: h=8, w=1, etc. So for i=16 (idx=256), h=8, w=0. This is contiguous in tile index but not contiguous in global memory (since h=7,w=31 to h=8,w=0 jumps by 1024-31 = 993 elements). But if we look at the elements loaded in iteration 1 (idx=240 to 479): Thread 0: idx=240, ic=0, h=7, w=16 Thread 1: idx=241, ic=0, h=7, w=17 ... Thread 15: idx=255, ic=0, h=7, w=31 Thread 16: idx=256, ic=0, h=8, w=0 Thread 17: idx=257, ic=0, h=8, w=1 ... For warp 0 (threads 0-31): Address of thread 15: base + 7*1024 + 31 = base + 7168 + 31 = base + 7199 Address of thread 16: base + 8*1024 + 0 = base + 8192 Gap = 8192 - 7199 = 993 elements = 3972 bytes. This spans about 62 cache lines of 64 bytes. NOT coalesced at all! So even with TILE_W=30, the problem remains: within a single warp loading elements with `idx = tid + k * nthreads`, the elements jump across rows. The fundamental issue with this mapping is: `tid` to `idx` gives contiguous idx, but contiguous idx in [ic][h][w] layout can span row boundaries, causing non-contiguous global memory accesses. The fix: Process the input tile by iterating over complete [ic][h] slices, where each slice has contiguous w values. For each (ic, h), there are 32 contiguous w values. We can load each (ic, h) slice with 32 threads (one warp), and each warp loads 32 contiguous floats. Implementation: ```cpp int total_slices = IC * in_tile_h; // Each slice is one row of in_tile_w elements int slice_len = in_tile_w; // = 32 for TILE_W=30 for (int slice_idx = tid / 32; slice_idx < total_slices; slice_idx += nthreads / 32) { int ic = slice_idx / in_tile_h; int h = slice_idx % in_tile_h; int lane = tid % 32; int w = lane; // Each lane loads one w if (w < slice_len) { int ih = oh_base + h; int iw = ow_base + w; float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && ih < H && iw < W) { val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_input[(ic * in_tile_h + h) * in_tile_w + w] = val; } } ``` In this implementation: - Threads 0-31 form warp 0. tid/32 = 0, lane = 0..31. They load slice 0: (ic=0, h=0), w=0..31. Global addresses: base + 0*1024 + 0 to base + 0*1024 + 31. Contiguous across the warp. Coalesced! - Threads 32-63 form warp 1. tid/32 = 1, lane = 0..31. They load slice 1: (ic=0, h=1), w=0..31. Global addresses: base + 1*1024 + 0 to base + 1*1024 + 31. Contiguous across the warp. Coalesced! This is beautiful! Each warp loads exactly one row of 32 contiguous floats. For a block with 240 threads, we have 240/32 = 7.5 warps. Actually 240 isn't a multiple of 32. 240 = 7*32 + 16. So 7 full warps + 1 partial warp of 16 threads. The partial warp loads slice 7: (ic=0, h=7), w=0..15. That's still fully coalesced (only 16 threads active). For weight loading, similar approach: Weight is [OC][IC][K][K]. For our tile, we load [TILE_OC][IC][K][K]. For fixed (oc, ic, kh), kw varies from 0..2. That's only 3 elements, not worth a whole warp. For weight, total elements = TILE_OC * IC * K * K = 32 * 16 * 9 = 4608. We can just linearize and have each thread load contiguous elements from global memory. Since weights in global memory are [OC][IC][K][K], and for fixed (oc, ic, kh), kw=0..2 are contiguous, then if we linearize in the same order, contiguous indices map to contiguous memory for the most part. Actually, with 4608 elements and 240 threads, each thread loads 19.2 elements. If we linearize with `idx = tid + k * nthreads`, thread 0 loads elements 0, 239, 478, ... which are NOT contiguous. But if the layout matches... Weight global layout: [oc][ic][kh][kw]. So (oc=0, ic=0, kh=0, kw=0) is at offset 0, kw=1 at offset 1, kw=2 at offset 2. Then kh=1 at offset 3, etc. So within a (oc, ic) block, 9 elements are contiguous. For warp coalescing, we want 32 threads to load 32 contiguous elements. But each (oc, ic) has only 9 elements. So 32 threads would span 3-4 (oc, ic) groups. The memory would be contiguous (since groups are adjacent in global memory), but there might be small gaps if we don't fill the last group completely. For simplicity, we can load weights the same way: each warp loads 32 contiguous elements using `float` loads. Since 4608 / 32 = 144 slices, and 4608 is divisible by 32, we have exactly 144 slices of 32 contiguous elements. ```cpp int total_weight_elems = TILE_OC * IC * K * K; for (int slice = tid / 32; slice < (total_weight_elems + 31) / 32; slice += nthreads / 32) { int lane = tid % 32; int idx = slice * 32 + lane; if (idx < total_weight_elems) { // decode idx to (oc, ic, kh, kw) int oc = idx / (IC * K * K); int rem = idx % (IC * K * K); int ic = rem / (K * K); rem = rem % (K * K); int kh = rem / K; int kw = rem % K; float val = 0.0f; if (oc_base + oc < OC) { val = weight[(((oc_base + oc) * IC + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } } ``` Each warp loads 32 contiguous floats from global memory (assuming weight layout matches our linearization). This is fully coalesced as long as there are no gaps. Since the total elements per (oc, ic) is 9, slice 0 covers (oc=0, ic=0, kh=0..2, kw=0..2) = 9 elements, then (oc=0, ic=0, kh=... wait, 9 elements for one (oc, ic). So slice 0 covers 3 full (oc, ic) groups + 5 elements of the 4th group. The groups are adjacent in memory, so a 32-element read spans ~4 groups with no gaps (each group is 9 contiguous floats). The warp loads a contiguous 128-byte region. Perfect! Actually wait, if idx=0 corresponds to (oc=0,ic=0,kh=0,kw=0) and idx=8 is (oc=0,ic=0,kh=2,kw=2), then idx=9 is (oc=0,ic=1,kh=0,kw=0). These ARE contiguous in memory! Because weight is stored as [oc][ic][kh][kw], so (oc=0, ic=0, all kh, kw) are followed by (oc=0, ic=1, all kh, kw). There's no gap. So yes, any contiguous range of idx maps to contiguous memory. The warp loads are fully coalesced. Great! This "slice" approach fixes coalescing for both input and weight loading. Now, for output writing: Each thread writes its computed output values. For TILE_W=30, in a 8x30 block: - Thread (ty, tx) writes output at (ow_base + tx, oh_base + ty). - A warp covers threads with consecutive tid. BlockDim.x = 30, so 30 threads per row. But warp size is 32! - Warp 0: threads 0..29 (ty=0, tx=0..29) and thread 30 (ty=1, tx=0). - This means the warp writes 30 values in row 0 (contiguous) and 1 value in row 1. This is NOT a clean coalescing pattern. To fix this, blockDim.x should be a multiple of 32! So 32, 64, etc. TILE_W = 30 gives blockDim.x = 30, which is not a multiple of 32. This breaks output coalescing. So let me go back to TILE_W = 32: Block = 8x32 = 256 threads. BlockDim.x = 32, which is exactly warp size. Warp 0: threads 0..31 → ty=0, tx=0..31. Writes row 0, columns 0..31. Perfect coalescing. Warp 1: threads 32..63 → ty=1, tx=0..31. Writes row 1. Perfect. For input loading with in_tile_w = 34: Using the slice approach, each warp loads one row of 32 elements (we ignore lanes >= 32). Wait, in_tile_w = 34, so a row has 34 elements. A warp of 32 threads can load 32 elements. The remaining 2 elements (w=32, 33) need another warp or partial warp. So we can have: ```cpp int total_slices = IC * in_tile_h * ((in_tile_w + 31) / 32); for (int slice = tid / 32; slice < total_slices; slice += nthreads / 32) { int lane = tid % 32; int w_group = slice % ((in_tile_w + 31) / 32); int slice_in_ic_h = slice / ((in_tile_w + 31) / 32); int ic = slice_in_ic_h / in_tile_h; int h = slice_in_ic_h % in_tile_h; int w = w_group * 32 + lane; if (w < in_tile_w) { // load from global to s_input } } ``` For in_tile_w=34: - w_group can be 0 or 1. - For w_group=0, w=0..31. Warp loads 32 contiguous floats. Coalesced. - For w_group=1, w=32..33. Only lanes 0..1 are active. Lanes 2..31 don't participate. This wastes some threads but is still coalesced (the active lanes access contiguous memory). For each (ic, h), we have 2 slices (32 + 2 elements). Total slices = 16 * 10 * 2 = 320 slices. With 8 warps (256 threads), 320/8 = 40 iterations. Each warp handles one slice per iteration. But each slice is a single global memory transaction (or 2 for w_group=0 since 32 floats = 128 bytes = 2 cache lines). For w_group=1, it's a tiny transaction. Actually, this seems pretty good. Let me write it out. Total elements loaded per block = 16*10*34 + 32*16*9 = 5440 + 4608 = 10,048 floats. With 256 threads, warps = 8. For input slices: 320 slices. Each warp does 320/8 = 40 slices. An SM has 4 schedulers, so it can execute 4 warps simultaneously in terms of instruction issue. But each warp executes on its own data. If all 8 warps are running on the same SM, they share 4 schedulers, so each scheduler handles 2 warps. Each warp has 4608 FMAs / (some IPC). With ILP of 32, each warp can issue FMAs back-to-back. So each scheduler can issue 1 FMA instruction per cycle (1 for each warp, alternating). 4 schedulers * 32 FMAs = 128 FMAs per cycle per SM. 8 warps * 4608 FMAs = 36,864 FMAs. At 128 FMAs/cycle, time for the SM to finish all 8 warps = 36,864 / 128 = 288 cycles. At 2GHz, 288 cycles = 144 ns per block! That's tiny! I'll use dynamic shared memory with `extern __shared__`. And I'll add the slice-based coalesced loading. For the tile dimensions, I'll start with TILE_H=8, TILE_W=32, TILE_OC=32. Actually, one more concern: with `extern __shared__`, the compiler doesn't know the size of s_input and s_weight at compile time for indexing optimizations. This is fine for static shared memory under 48KB. With template parameters, I can even make the sizes template-dependent: ```cpp template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC, int K_VAL, int IC_VAL> __global__ void conv2d_kernel(...) { const int IN_TILE_H = TILE_H + K_VAL - 1; const int IN_TILE_W = TILE_W + K_VAL - 1; __shared__ float s_input[IC_VAL * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W]; __shared__ float s_weight[TILE_OC * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL]; ... } ``` For TILE_H=8, TILE_W=32, TILE_OC=32, K_VAL=3, IC_VAL=16: s_input = 16*10*34 = 5440 floats = 21,760 bytes s_weight = 32*16*9 = 4608 floats = 18,432 bytes Total static shared = 40,192 bytes = 39.25 KB. Is this under the default static shared memory limit? For Hopper, I believe the limit might be 48KB. Let me verify. On Ampere (SM80), the shared memory limit per block is 164KB or 228KB with `cudaFuncSetAttribute`. But some sources say the default without setting attributes is 48KB. Actually, I think the architecture supports up to 164/228KB, but the default max is 48KB for backward compatibility unless you explicitly increase it. On newer architectures, there might not be a 48KB compile-time limit, but rather a runtime limit based on occupancy. To be safe, I could use `extern __shared__` and pass the size at launch. But then I need `cudaFuncSetAttribute` for >48KB. At 39KB, I'm under 48KB, so it should be fine with static shared memory. Let me try static and see if it compiles. Actually, I should use `extern` to be safe and since dynamic shared memory allows more flexibility. For the first attempt, I'll use `extern __shared__ float s_mem[];` and slice it. Now, I also need a generic kernel as fallback for cases where K != 3 or IC != 16. I'll write a simple generic kernel that uses the same approach but without unrolling and without compile-time constants. Let me draft the complete C++ code. In the wrapper function `conv2d_forward`: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation, int64_t groups) { // input: [N, IC, H, W] // weight: [OC, IC/groups, K, K] int N = input.size(0); int IC = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); int OC = weight.size(0); int K = weight.size(2); // Calculate output dims int OH = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; int OW = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1; // ... handle stride, padding, dilation auto output = torch::empty({N, OC, OH, OW}, input.options()); if (K == 3 && stride == 1 && padding == 0 && dilation == 1 && groups == 1 && IC == 16) { const int TILE_H = 8; const int TILE_W = 32; const int TILE_OC = 32; dim3 block(TILE_W, TILE_H); dim3 grid((OW + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W, (OH + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H, N * ((OC + TILE_OC - 1) / TILE_OC)); size_t smem = (IC * (TILE_H + K - 1) * (TILE_W + K - 1) + TILE_OC * IC * K * K) * sizeof(float); conv2d_kernel_specialized<TILE_H, TILE_W, TILE_OC, 3, 16><<<grid, block, smem>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, OC, H, W, OH, OW); } else { // Generic fallback - we could call at::conv2d or implement a generic kernel output = at::conv2d(input, weight, bias, {stride, stride}, {padding, padding}, {dilation, dilation}, groups); } return output; } ``` Wait, actually to match the constructor args, we might want to just call at::conv2d for the generic case. But the task says to write a custom CUDA kernel. It's probably fine to fall back to at::conv2d for unsupported parameters. Also, for the test, the parameters are K=3, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1, groups=1, IC=16. So our specialized kernel will be used. Now let me think about whether padding will be applied in our kernel. The test has padding=0. If padding > 0, we need to handle it in the window coordinates. For the generic fallback, at::conv2d handles it. For the specialized kernel, we can add support or fall back. Since our test has padding=0, and we're focusing on the test case, I'll add padding support in the generic fallback but for the specialized kernel, I'll just compute the reference if padding is non-zero. Same for stride and dilation. Let me write the kernel more carefully. First, the specialized kernel with slice-based coalesced loading: ```cpp template<int TILE_H, int TILE_W, int TILE_OC, int K_VAL, int IC_VAL> __global__ void conv2d_specialized( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int OC, int H, int W, int OH, int OW) { const int IN_TILE_H = TILE_H + K_VAL - 1; const int IN_TILE_W = TILE_W + K_VAL - 1; int n = blockIdx.z / ((OC + TILE_OC - 1) / TILE_OC); int oc_block = blockIdx.z % ((OC + TILE_OC - 1) / TILE_OC); int oh_base = blockIdx.y * TILE_H; int ow_base = blockIdx.x * TILE_W; int oc_base = oc_block * TILE_OC; int tx = threadIdx.x; int ty = threadIdx.y; int tid = ty * blockDim.x + tx; int nthreads = blockDim.x * blockDim.y; int num_warps = nthreads / 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane_id = tid % 32; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_input = s_mem; float* s_weight = s_mem + IC_VAL * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W; // Load input with slice-based coalescing int input_slices_per_row = (IN_TILE_W + 31) / 32; int total_input_slices = IC_VAL * IN_TILE_H * input_slices_per_row; for (int slice = warp_id; slice < total_input_slices; slice += num_warps) { int tmp = slice / input_slices_per_row; int w_group = slice % input_slices_per_row; int ic = tmp / IN_TILE_H; int ih_local = tmp % IN_TILE_H; int iw_local = w_group * 32 + lane_id; if (iw_local < IN_TILE_W) { int ih = oh_base + ih_local; int iw = ow_base + iw_local; float val = 0.0f; if (n < N && ih < H && iw < W) { val = input[((n * IC_VAL + ic) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } s_input[(ic * IN_TILE_H + ih_local) * IN_TILE_W + iw_local] = val; } } // Load weights int weight_total = TILE_OC * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL; int weight_slices = (weight_total + 31) / 32; for (int slice = warp_id; slice < weight_slices; slice += num_warps) { int idx = slice * 32 + lane_id; if (idx < weight_total) { int oc = idx / (IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL); int rem = idx % (IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL); int ic = rem / (K_VAL * K_VAL); rem = rem % (K_VAL * K_VAL); int kh = rem / K_VAL; int kw = rem % K_VAL; float val = 0.0f; if (oc_base + oc < OC) { val = weight[(((oc_base + oc) * IC_VAL + ic) * K_VAL + kh) * K_VAL + kw]; } s_weight[idx] = val; } } __syncthreads(); int oh = oh_base + ty; int ow = ow_base + tx; float accum[TILE_OC]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < TILE_OC; i++) { accum[i] = 0.0f; } if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K_VAL; kh++) { #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K_VAL; kw++) { int ih_local = ty + kh; int iw_local = tx + kw; int in_idx = ih_local * IN_TILE_W + iw_local; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < IC_VAL; ic++) { float in_val = s_input[ic * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W + in_idx]; #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { float w_val = s_weight[oc * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL + ic * K_VAL * K_VAL + kh * K_VAL + kw]; accum[oc] += w_val * in_val; } } } } } // Store output if (oh < OH && ow < OW) { #pragma unroll for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { if (oc_base + oc < OC) { float val = accum[oc]; if (bias != nullptr) { val += bias[oc_base + oc]; } output[(((n * OC + oc_base + oc) * OH + oh) * OW + ow)] = val; } } } } ``` Wait, if IC_VAL and K_VAL are compile-time constants, then the compiler CAN unroll the loops. Good. One issue: `weight_slices` computation at runtime. The loop `for (int slice = warp_id; slice < weight_slices; slice += num_warps)` can't be unrolled because `weight_slices` is a runtime constant derived from template parameters... but actually `weight_slices = (TILE_OC * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL + 31) / 32` which IS a compile-time constant if all are template parameters! Yes, since TILE_OC, IC_VAL, K_VAL are all compile-time constants, `weight_total` and `weight_slices` are also compile-time constants. The compiler can unroll these loops too! Similarly, `total_input_slices = IC_VAL * IN_TILE_H * input_slices_per_row` where `IN_TILE_H = TILE_H + K_VAL - 1` and `input_slices_per_row = (IN_TILE_W + 31) / 32`. These are all compile-time constants. So the loading loops can be unrolled too! This is excellent. The compiler should generate unrolled code for the entire loading and compute phases. One more optimization: for output writing, we currently do: ```cpp for (int oc = 0; oc < TILE_OC; oc++) { output[...] = val; } ``` Since `oc_base + oc < OC` is usually true for all oc (except possibly the last block if OC is not divisible by TILE_OC), and in our test OC=128, TILE_OC=32, so it's always true. The compiler might optimize away the branch. But what if we write output with fewer stores? We can't use vectorized stores easily since different oc are not contiguous. But we could process oc=0..31 and store sequentially. The compiler will probably generate 32 `STG` instructions. One thought: instead of having each thread compute all 32 OCs, we could have warps specialize in subsets of OCs. For example, warp 0 computes oc=0..7, warp 1 computes oc=8..15, etc. Then each thread has fewer accumulators but we could interleave compute across warps. But with 32 accumulators already, the FMA pipeline should be well-utilized. Let me also consider output layout. Output is NCHW: [N][OC][OH][OW]. For fixed n, oc, the spatial pixels are contiguous along W. We write pixel (oh, ow). Since adjacent threads in a warp (same warp for blockDim.x=32) write adjacent W for the same OC, each store is coalesced. Now, the only issue with 32 stores per thread is instruction overhead. 32 STG instructions per thread. With 256 threads, that's 8192 stores per block. For 262K blocks, about 2.1B stores. Sounds like a lot, but it's the correct number since we have 2.14B output values. Each output value is written exactly once. Wait, 2.14B outputs / 256 threads = 8.4M stores per thread? No, each thread writes 32 values, but over many blocks. Total stores = 2.14B. The total store bandwidth needed = 8.56GB. At 3.35TB/s, that's negligible compared to compute. OK, this all seems reasonable. Let me write the complete code. One thing I forgot: in the weight loading, the layout in shared memory is [oc][ic][kh][kw]. But in the compute loop, we access as: ```cpp s_weight[oc * IC_VAL * K_VAL * K_VAL + ic * K_VAL * K_VAL + kh * K_VAL + kw] ``` This matches the linearization. Good. During compute: ```cpp int in_idx = ih_local * IN_TILE_W + iw_local; float in_val = s_input[ic * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W + in_idx]; ``` This is correct. reasoning
The issue is that `load_inline` requires both `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` to be defined, or at least have a function declaration in cpp_sources. During compilation, the cpp side tries to find the function declaration but can't since it's only in the cuda source. I need to provide a forward declaration in `cpp_sources` so that PyTorch's wrapper can find it. Let me fix this.

import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline

cpp_sources = """
torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor weight,
    c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias_opt,
    int64_t stride,
    int64_t padding,
    int64_t dilation,
    int64_t groups);
"""

cuda_source = """
#include <torch/extension.h>
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

__global__ void conv2d_kernel_v2(
    const float* __restrict__ input,
    const float* __restrict__ weight,
    float* __restrict__ output,
    int N, int OC, int IC, int H, int W, int OH, int OW) {
    
    const int K = 3;
    const int TILE_H = 8;
    const int TILE_W = 32;
    const int IN_TILE_H = TILE_H + K - 1;  // 10
    const int IN_TILE_W = TILE_W + K - 1;  // 34
    const int OC_BLOCK = 32;
    
    int n = blockIdx.z;
    int oc_block = blockIdx.y;
    int oh_base = (blockIdx.x / ((OW + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W)) * TILE_H;
    int ow_base = (blockIdx.x % ((OW + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W)) * TILE_W;
    int oc_base = oc_block * OC_BLOCK;
    
    int tid = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;
    int lane = tid % 32;
    int warp = tid / 32;
    int numWarps = blockDim.x * blockDim.y / 32;
    
    __shared__ float s_input[16 * 10 * 34];
    __shared__ float s_weight[32 * 16 * 3 * 3];
    
    // Load input cooperatively
    int inTotal = IC * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W;
    for (int i = warp * 32 + lane; i < inTotal; i += numWarps * 32) {
        int ic = i / (IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W);
        int rem = i % (IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W);
        int ih = rem / IN_TILE_W;
        int iw = rem % IN_TILE_W;
        
        int global_h = oh_base + ih;
        int global_w = ow_base + iw;
        float val = 0.0f;
        if (n < N && global_h < H && global_w < W) {
            val = input[((n * IC + ic) * H + global_h) * W + global_w];
        }
        s_input[i] = val;
    }
    
    // Load weight cooperatively
    int wTotal = OC_BLOCK * IC * K * K;
    for (int i = warp * 32 + lane; i < wTotal; i += numWarps * 32) {
        int oc = i / (IC * K * K);
        int rem = i % (IC * K * K);
        int ic = rem / (K * K);
        int rem2 = rem % (K * K);
        int kh = rem2 / K;
        int kw = rem2 % K;
        
        float val = 0.0f;
        if (oc_base + oc < OC) {
            val = weight[(((oc_base + oc) * IC + ic) * K + kh) * K + kw];
        }
        s_weight[i] = val;
    }
    
    __syncthreads();
    
    int oh = oh_base + threadIdx.y;
    int ow = ow_base + threadIdx.x;
    
    float accum[32];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < 32; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f;
    
    if (oh < OH && ow < OW) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) {
                int ih = threadIdx.y + kh;
                int iw = threadIdx.x + kw;
                int in_idx = ih * IN_TILE_W + iw;
                
                #pragma unroll
                for (int ic = 0; ic < 16; ic++) {
                    float in_val = s_input[ic * IN_TILE_H * IN_TILE_W + in_idx];
                    
                    #pragma unroll
                    for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; oc++) {
                        float w_val = s_weight[((oc * 16 + ic) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw];
                        accum[oc] += w_val * in_val;
                    }
                }
            }
        }
    }
    
    if (oh < OH && ow < OW) {
        #pragma unroll
        for (int oc = 0; oc < 32; oc++) {
            if (oc_base + oc < OC) {
                output[(((n * OC + oc_base + oc) * OH + oh) * OW + ow)] = accum[oc];
            }
        }
    }
}

torch::Tensor conv2d_forward(
    torch::Tensor input,
    torch::Tensor weight,
    c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias_opt,
    int64_t stride,
    int64_t padding,
    int64_t dilation,
    int64_t groups) {
    
    int N = input.size(0);
    int IC = input.size(1);
    int H = input.size(2);
    int W = input.size(3);
    int OC = weight.size(0);
    int K = weight.size(2);
    
    int OH = (H + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1;
    int OW = (W + 2 * padding - dilation * (K - 1) - 1) / stride + 1;
    
    auto output = torch::empty({N, OC, OH, OW}, input.options());
    
    if (K == 3 && stride == 1 && padding == 0 && dilation == 1 && groups == 1 && IC == 16) {
        dim3 block(32, 8);
        int grid_x = ((OW + 31) / 32) * ((OH + 7) / 8);
        int grid_y = (OC + 31) / 32;
        int grid_z = N;
        dim3 grid(grid_x, grid_y, grid_z);
        
        conv2d_kernel_v2<<<grid, block>>>(
            input.data_ptr<float>(),
            weight.data_ptr<float>(),
            output.data_ptr<float>(),
            N, OC, IC, H, W, OH, OW
        );
    } else {
        at::Tensor bias_tensor = bias_opt.has_value() ? bias_opt.value() : at::Tensor();
        output = at::conv2d(input, weight, bias_tensor, {stride, stride}, {padding, padding}, {dilation, dilation}, groups);
    }
    
    return output;
}
"""

module = load_inline(
    name='conv2d_optimized',
    cpp_sources=cpp_sources,
    cuda_sources=cuda_source,
    functions=['conv2d_forward'],
    extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'],
    verbose=False
)

class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(ModelNew, self).__init__()
        self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, (kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias)
    
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        return module.conv2d_forward(
            x, 
            self.conv2d.weight, 
            self.conv2d.bias if self.conv2d.bias is not None else None,
            self.conv2d.stride[0],
            self.conv2d.padding[0],
            self.conv2d.dilation[0],
            self.conv2d.groups
        )
